The extent of the man&rsquo;s injuries were unknown, though Fire-Rescue issued a trauma alert for him, meaning that the hospital&rsquo;s trauma ward would be prepared for his arrival. &ldquo;Trauma alerts are always life-threatening,&rdquo; Marx said at the scene. He added that the man had &ldquo;pretty extensive injuries.&rdquo; A supervisor at the scene said he and the man who fell work for Euro Homes Inc., a Palm Beach contractor. Zack Ciomek of Euro Homes declined to comment when reached by phone. According to Palm Beach County property records, 476 S. Ocean Blvd. is owned by a trust. The owners couldn&rsquo;t be reached for comment. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/fall-down-elevator-shaft-palm-beach-home-sends-worker-hospital/cKFUKQ9c4BsRuj3fmCbeQL/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/21/Images/newsEngin.18487772_20170421-PBDN-ELEVATOR-FALL-CT-01_web.jpg\" title=\"Fall down elevator shaft at Palm Beach home sends worker to hospital \" alt=\"Fall down elevator shaft at Palm Beach home sends worker to hospital \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/fall-down-elevator-shaft-palm-beach-home-sends-worker-hospital/cKFUKQ9c4BsRuj3fmCbeQL/\" target=\"\">Fall down elevator shaft at Palm Beach home sends worker to hospital </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nEleanor Roy, <span class=\"story-source\">Daily News Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>A worker was taken to the hospital Friday after he fell down a 20-foot elevator shaft, according to Palm Beach Fire-Rescue. Here are two dogs who travel with them, and one is so travel-savvy he has been named &ldquo;The World&rsquo;s Most Traveled Dog.&rdquo; * Six years ago  Sally Ann Nisberg was diagnosed with a rare, triple negative breast cancer. After three years of grueling treatments and surgeries, she decided to help others undergoing medical traumas by writing a book reflecting her own philosophy after the ordeal: &ldquo;Live Like Crazy: My Will to Heal in the Wake of Breast Cancer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Live Like Crazy&rdquo; is still her motto. She and her husband, Jay Nisberg, a management consultant, regularly leave their North End home to travel north, always taking their 3-year old Portuguese water dog, Haley, in their Ranch Rover with them. Together, they stop in pet-friendly lodgings in place such as Savannah, Charleston and Asheville, through to Connecticut to see their family (three children and four grandchildren) and finally to their second home in Vermont. Haley can&rsquo;t wait. When the Nisbergs approach their car, Harley dashes to the backseat door to make sure they won&rsquo;t forget him. Fat chance! Soon, he&rsquo;ll be luxuriating on his quilted cover, and when they stop, he&rsquo;ll charm everyone he meets along the way. &ldquo;Everyone stops us,&rdquo; said Sally. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s an unusual brown color, and most Portuguese water dogs are black. But probably the only ones who have sailed on it 25 times are a dog named Coco and her owner, Cynthia Ivana Popovic, an emergency nurse. Not only has the 17-year-old Morky (Maltese/Yorkie) sailed 25 times, but also she sailed earlier on the QE II 10 times. That&rsquo;s not special because &ldquo;every captain has come to visit Coco,&rdquo; said Cynthia. \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  If there&rsquo;s a song in your heart waiting to get out, you might want to sign up for The Society of the Four Arts&rsquo; choral workshop, scheduled for 6 p.m. Tuesday in the Dixon Education Building. The free workshop will be led by Donna Plasket, wife of Four Arts president and chief executive officer David Breneman, and Phillip Bergmann, head of music and film programming. Plasket is a choral conductor and educator. Bergmann is a pianist with a degree in vocal performance. Once people learned about Plasket&rsquo;s background in choral music they began asking her to put it to use at the Four Arts, she said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re not trying to create a chorus,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;This is just a chance for people to come to the Four Arts and make music together.&rdquo; Many people have sung in choruses earlier in their lives and want to get back into it, she said. As of Wednesday, 44 people had signed up. The workshop will include warm-ups, vocal exercises and reading through choral scores, although it&rsquo;s not necessary to be able to sight-read music to participate. The music will be choral standards that most singers are familiar with. Whether the workshop develops something more depends on the skills and desires of the singers who show up. &ldquo;If we get the sense that people want to do this on a more regular basis, we&rsquo;re open,&rdquo; Plasket said. Reservations are required. But he also likes to clean it. The Florida Wildlife Federation is honoring Foster, a ninth-grader at the Palm Beach Day Academy, with the Youth Conservationist Award for his dedication and efforts in the community while protecting Florida&rsquo;s beaches and marine life. Foster, 15, has been involved in efforts to ban harmful waste such as balloons, bags and plastic. &ldquo;I was always spending so much time in the water,&rdquo; said Foster, who likes to body-board, paddle-board and surf. &ldquo;I wanted to give back.&rdquo; He&rsquo;s also been involved in local beach cleanups. &ldquo;The hardest part to clean up was the plastic bags, because they would turn into spaghetti when they start to pull apart,&rdquo; Foster said. He felt nervous speaking before a large group but was confident in his research. Florida doesn&rsquo;t allow any of its local governments to ban the use of plastic bags, prompting the council to adopt a resolution in January asking the Florida Legislature to repeal the statute. Other purely tropical Atlantic systems in April were: 1 tropical depression in 1973, 2 tropical depressions in 1981 and Ana in 2003. &mdash; Philip Klotzbach (@philklotzbach) April 20, 2017 This is the second consecutive year where hurricane season started early. The incredibly rare storm that gained hurricane status hundreds of miles south of the Azores, was called &ldquo;remarkable&rdquo; by National Hurricane Center forecasters who noted that since 1851, just one previous hurricane has formed in January. \n<!-- map content:  Audubon Florida received a town permit Tuesday to plant native species on Bingham Island and the group is getting right to work. An Audubon contractor recently removed invasive species on the island, which is located just south of the Southern Boulevard Causeway. Now the group will fill the clearing with native plants. Planting will start Saturday morning in honor of Earth Day, said Campion Platt, who is part of a group of residents leading efforts and raising money to restore the area to its original purpose as a sanctuary and nature reserve. The Town Council is in the process of modifying its permitting and oversight regulations in conservation districts but agreed last week to give staff permission to issue the Audubon permit in advance. The group needs to plant now before the state begins setting up equipment for work on the Southern Boulevard Bridge, officials have said. And summer is a good growing season for native plants. A landscaping contractor plans to plant seagrape, buttonwood, mangrove, blolly, gumbo-limbo and wild lime trees, as well as several varieties of shrubs. &ldquo;They now intend to plant new, native species, especially along the shoreline,&rdquo; Planning, Zoning and Building Director John Page told the council last week. &ldquo;They have submitted a very detailed plan to the town that has been reviewed. We feel very comfortable with the plan.&rdquo; There had been a misunderstanding last month that the Audubon damaged and removed mangroves. The Audubon denied those claims. The town questioned the Florida Department of Environmental Protection about the claims and state officials said impacts to mangroves were &ldquo;de minimis,&rdquo; and lauded the Audubon&rsquo;s clearing efforts. Platt attended the council meeting last week to explain the Audubon  project. Councilwoman Bobbie Lindsay thanked Platt for &ldquo;clearing the air. &hellip; I think now that we have the facts I&rsquo;m very comfortable proceeding as I believe Audubon and the town are aligned in terms of their approach to dealing with this conservation area.&rdquo; Regulations coming While the Audubon project proceeds, the town is working to create better oversight on future conservation projects. The council approved first reading of an ordinance that makes it unlawful for a person or group to remove, relocate or plant landscape in conservation districts without a permit. The council&rsquo;s Ordinances, Rules and Standards Committee still is reviewing the details of that permit. Platt said the Audubon asked him to make clear &ldquo;that there are costs associated with restoration and that extraordinary restrictions to the restoration efforts may limit the effectiveness of the overall effort.&rdquo; Mayor Gail Coniglio said she thinks both sides are on the same page in wanting to restore the island&rsquo;s to their natural beauty, but said the town should have some &ldquo;monitoring ability.&rdquo; Palm Beach and Evanston, Ill., died Sunday, April 16, 2017. Mr. Flynn was born in Montreal, the son of Emmett and Pauline Flynn. He joined the U.S. Marine Corps Reserve in 1958 and became a naturalized U.S. citizen in 1962. After earning a bachelor of science degree from Loyola College, a bachelor of engineering degree from McGill University and a masters of business administration from Rutgers University, he began his business career. During his career, he was senior vice president of The Carborundum Co. in New York and chairman and chief executive officer of Fisher Controls International, Inc., in Missouri. He retired in 1995 as the chairman and chief executive officer of The NutraSweet Co. in Illinois. Mr. Flynn served on the board of directors for numerous corporate and non-profit entities, including the United Way, the North Shore University Health System and STANTEC. He is survived by his wife of 57 years, Irene (Kantor) Flynn; two daughters, Donna Flynn Page and husband Charles, and Diane Flynn Pollard and husband Vernon; a son, Darren Robert Flynn and his wife, Roshni; a grandson, Andrew Flynn; a sister, Diana (Callahan) Hocutt; and numerous nieces and nephews. The family will receive friends from 9:30 a.m. until the funeral at 10:30 a.m. Saturday at the Donnellan Family Funeral Home, 10045 Skokie Blvd., Skokie, Ill. In lieu of flowers, memorial donations may be made to: PAWS Chicago, 1997 N. \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n \n<!-- map content:  The Chamber Music Society of the Palm Beaches concluded its 2016-17 season in style with a brilliant piano quartet performance, featuring the happy return of Young Artist Series alum, violinist Paul Huang. Joining Huang in the Mar-a-Lago ballroom were violist Toby Appel, cellist Edward Arron and pianist Orion Weiss. The program featured two works that helped establish the piano quartet as a serious genre. The concert opened with Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart&rsquo;s Piano Quartet No. 2 in E-flat major, K. 493, and concluded with Johannes Brahms&rsquo;s Quartet No. 1 in G Minor, Op. 25. As he did with the piano trio, Mozart brought the quartet out of the realm of the amateur by increasing the demands on the players and increasing its length. The E-flat quartet is mature Mozart, featuring his deft balance of emerging Romantic seriousness, tempered with the Italian charm that was so popular in his day. Immediately, one can hear why Huang is a rising star. His tone was warm and balanced, even in the rather dry acoustic of the Mar-a-Lago setting. He has all the technique one would expect from the Lincoln Center&rsquo;s Emerging Artist of the Year. Yet, while Huang organized the quartet, the leader of the Mozart was pianist Weiss, whose career is just as promising as Huang&rsquo;s. His approach to Mozart was simply perfect. He was able to recreate the natural separation one hears in pianofortes of Mozart&rsquo;s time while still making use of the dynamic possibilities of the modern instrument. Composed during the late 1850s, the Brahms G-Minor Quartet was premiered with Clara Schumann at the piano in 1861. This work further pushed the boundaries of the genre, both in length and in number of ideas it presents. Of all his chamber works, however, this one shows off the jolly face of Brahms the most. This is most evident in the quirky intermezzo movement that featured the violin and viola muted throughout. The most fun occurred in the finale, which had the ensemble zipping through a catchy Gypsy tune at breathtaking speed. The Brahms quartet is a monumental work that may have benefited from a larger ensemble. Brahms frequently reconsidered the medium for his works, after all. This reaching for more sonority was evident in the performance during the moments that the ensemble took their instruments slightly beyond their sonic limits in volume. This may have been the result of the hall&rsquo;s acoustic nature, especially with a full house. As an ensemble, the Huang-Appel-Arron-Weiss Quartet (they really should come up with a good name) works well, but needs a few years for each member to carve out his musical space. There is no doubt about the splendid musicianship of the players, but one does not sense an intimate interaction between them just yet. Perhaps this is not a permanent ensemble, but if it is to be, it certainly has a very bright future. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/events/performing-arts/review-violinist-leads-quartet-grand-season-finale-mar-lago/Jnfkz2tawlk4zQTgBfPMkN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/20/Images/newsEngin.18469343_web-huang.jpg\" title=\"Review: Violinist leads quartet in grand season finale at Mar-a-Lago \" alt=\"Review: Violinist leads quartet in grand season finale at Mar-a-Lago \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/events/performing-arts/review-violinist-leads-quartet-grand-season-finale-mar-lago/Jnfkz2tawlk4zQTgBfPMkN/\" target=\"\">Review: Violinist leads quartet in grand season finale at Mar-a-Lago </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nMICHAEL O'CONNOR, <span class=\"story-source\">Special to the Daily News</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate is-truncated\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The Chamber Music Society of the Palm Beaches concluded its 2016-17 season in style with a brilliant piano quartet performance, featuring the happy return of Young Artist Series alum, violinist Paul Huang. \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  The National Hurricane Center identified Subtropical Depression One on Wednesday expecting a short-lived life in the far off Central Atlantic. But in a twist, the system turned fully tropical today with a more symmetric grouping of thunderstorms and 35 mph winds &ndash; four miles below tropical storm status. The system is no threat to the U.S., but is notable for its spin up this early in the season. Other purely tropical Atlantic systems in April were: 1 tropical depression in 1973, 2 tropical depressions in 1981 and Ana in 2003. &mdash; Philip Klotzbach (@philklotzbach) April 20, 2017 It would be named Arlene if it reaches tropical storm force, which isn&rsquo;t expected as it moves northwest at 14 mph about 730 miles west of the Azores. So what does it matter that the system morphed from sub-tropical to tropical. &ldquo;It doesn&rsquo;t mean a thing in the world for the most part,&rdquo; said Max Mayfield, a former director of the National Hurricane Center and hurricane specialist for Channel 10 in Miami. Technically, subtropical systems have their strongest winds further from the center and don&rsquo;t have a fully warm core. Tropical cyclones have their strongest winds at the center and are fueled by a warm core that draws energy from evaporation of warm ocean waters. Mayfield, who is attending the National Hurricane Conference in New Orleans, said especially because the depression is no threat to land, the distinction between subtropical and tropical isn&rsquo;t a big deal. But he said it is a testament to how far technology has come that forecasters can make the observations so far out to sea. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story \">\n\t    <div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"http://weatherplus.blog.palmbeachdailynews.com/2017/04/20/first-depression-of-season-turns-fully-tropical-what-it-means/\" target=\"\">First depression of season turns fully tropical, what it means </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nStaff Writer</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The National Hurricane Center identified Subtropical Depression One on Wednesday expecting a short-lived life in the far off Central Atlantic. -->\n        <!-- cacheScope: ContextMap[_rootContainerContextMap:ContextMap[primaryCategory:/Online/Home, sectionId:/]] -->\n        <!-- cacheScope type: class System will usher in Holocaust Remembrance Day, known as Yom HaShoah, with stories from people who carry its scars. Survivors will be honored and messages for future generations shared. &ldquo;As they share their lives with the younger generation, they carry the message of ensuring that bigotry and hatred have no place in our society,&rdquo; said Alan Sherman, chaplain for MorseLife. Hal Gottschall\n            , a board member of Alpert Jewish Family and Children&rsquo;s Service, will discuss his Holocaust experiences at the 9:15 a.m. service Saturday at Temple Emanu-El of Palm Beach, 190 N. County Road. Gottschall was hidden from 1943 to 1945 with a Christian family in the Hague, Holland, during the Holocaust, while his mom was hidden in Amsterdam. &ldquo;I am one of the very lucky ones,&rdquo; said Gottschall. &ldquo;If there are more families &hellip; who have the moral integrity and courage to do the right thing, no matter the consequences, then there is hope.&rdquo; Upon liberation by American soldiers, he recalls the joy at freedom and in revealing he was Jewish. &ldquo;My foster father gave me a Dutch flag. When I heard trucks arrive, I took the flag and ran outside in my underwear and yelled, &lsquo;I am a Jewish boy. I am a Jewish boy.&rsquo;&rdquo; He was reunited with his mom shortly thereafter. On Sunday, teens from Temple Emanu-El will partner with Holocaust survivors speaking at MorseLife&rsquo;s Tradition of the Palm Beaches in West Palm Beach, to honor and learn more about them. There will be dinner and a memorial candle-lighting ceremony, said Rabbi Matan Peled, director of Temple Emanu-El youth education and family programming, whose grandfather was survivor of concentration camps. The event will begin at 7:30 p.m. with guest speaker Randi Posner of Next Generations, which promotes Holocaust education and the well-being of survivors in need. Also speaking will be Fedora Cohn Horowitz, who was 5 during the Romanian Holocaust. Her father was one of the few survivors of a &ldquo;death train&rdquo; in Iasi, 1941, during which Jews were jammed into cattle cars and the air crevices sealed. Years later, her family suffered under Romania&rsquo;s oppressive communist regime. They escaped in 1958 by fleeing to Israel. She and her husband Avraham settled in the United States in 1969. &ldquo;The death of my father, more than 40 years ago, triggered the impulse to share the memories of my life during World War II and the Romanian Holocaust,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;I took an oath to never forget and to impart my feelings and knowledge with the younger generation.&rdquo; A concert pianist and former university music instructor, she also has written about the Holocaust. Her memoir is titled \n               Only Yesterday, 1941-1958\n            . Prayers and music will be led by Sherman and cantorial soloist Margaret Schmitt. The events are free and open to the public. To RSVP for The Tradition program, call 687-5749. * Helping others &mdash; \n               Royal Poinciana Kids\n             will present a barbecue fundraiser at noon Sunday at the chapel. Proceeds will go toward purchasing a home for a family in Haiti. &ldquo;We have chosen as Food for the Poor in Haiti as our mission outreach for the year,&rdquo; said Ashley Crandall, youth ministry director. The children also have raised money to buy cows and solar panels for people in Haiti. A sandal drive and lollipop collection likewise benefited Haitians. &ldquo;We talk about how we can help the less fortunate,&rdquo; Crandall said, noting that parents of some children visit Haiti to help. To buy tickets to the barbecue, call 655-4212. * Guest minister &mdash; \n               Paramount Church\n             will welcome \n               Raphael Giglio\n             to preach at its 10:30 a.m. service Sunday. The senior pastor leads worship and teaches the Bible at Zarephath Christian Church in New Jersey and is a veteran in the Nashville music industry, both as a recording artist and record company executive. His topic will be: &ldquo;Vision and Hope for a New Season of Your Life.&rdquo; * Medical series &mdash; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the best way to ensure you receive the medical care you want?&rdquo; is topic of a lunch and learn starting at 11:30 a.m. Wednesday at Temple Israel, 1901 N. Flagler Drive, West Palm Beach. Dr. \n               Stuart Bagatelle, program director of the Internal Medicine Residency Program at the University of Miami/JFK, will speak about this and &ldquo;Physician&rsquo;s Orders for Life-Sustaining Treatments.&rdquo; The lunch costs $5 with an RSVP.   \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  It&rsquo;s Thursday and once again it&rsquo;s time for #TBT, Throwback Thursday the Donald Trump edition. Today, it&rsquo;s appropriate to remember the flag flap with Palm Beach. Here&rsquo;s how I described it 10 years ago: &ldquo;A super-sized Star-Spangled Banner at Mar-a-Lago waved at the center of a six-month standoff between Donald Trump and the town. By the time it was all over, the 375-square-foot flag, towering on an 80-foot pole, seemed nearly as famous as the real estate mogul who put it there.&rdquo; On April 20, 2007, the Palm Beach Town Council held a meeting regarding the Mar-a-Lago flagpole lawsuit. A closed-door session was set for the next day over the case. Then-Mayor Jack McDonald, the Town Council and Town Attorney John Randolph were expected to discuss &ldquo;settlement negotiations and/or strategy relating to litigation and expenses&rdquo; in the case. The background: Trump had an 80-foot flagpole installed in the front setback of the private club in October 2006. The work was done without a permit, and the height and location of the pole violated town ordinances. He sued the town in December 2006 for $10 million over repeated demands that he withdraw the pole. He later increased the damage demand to $25 million. The town began fining Trump $1,250 a day on Jan. 6, 2007, for the code violations. So how did the meeting come out? Trump and Palm Beach agreed to settle the lawsuit that weekend. (Trump wasn&rsquo;t there, though.) As part of the pact, Trump was to erect a 70-foot flagpole farther inland on the front lawn of his estate and the town would waive the $120,000 in code fines. Trump also was to contribute $100,000 to charities that deal with Iraq war veterans or the U.S. flag or to the local veterans&rsquo; hospital. The flap officially ended on April 27, 2007, when the two sides signed off on a settlement that waived the fines and killed the lawsuit. versions to gather dust in the car&rsquo;s glove box. But as I travel around the world these days, I find that maps &mdash; where they chart land or sea &mdash; are finding their way onto fabrics, wallpapers and ceilings, as well as coffee tables and end tables. Last weekend, friends and I dined at one of the island&rsquo;s newest bistros, Costa Palm Beach in The Esplanade at 220 Worth Ave., the building that houses some of the town&rsquo;s finest boutiques. In fact, we dined at a table on the balcony and could see shop windows that spotlighted handbags on which were leather-cutout images of my favorite black-and-white Staffordshire dogs. Honestly, though, I prefer seeing the classic dog figurines displayed on shelves or on mantels. Just my taste, of course. I enjoyed both the dinner conversation at my table and the Mediterranean seafood tapas &mdash; gambas al ajillo, or shrimp cooked in garlic-spiced olive oil. One of the walls in the restaurant has been silhouette-painted with images of the European continent, and all the major seaport cities and towns duly noted. Portofino, Milan, Nice, Cannes, Madrid &mdash; yes, the maps transport you to all the ports of call, where delicacies from the sea are served at beachside and wharf cafes. In the men&rsquo;s room, I saw more walls with maps of the world, concentrating on the Mediterranean. Costa is co-owned and operated by Sascha Bennemann and his wife, Danielle. It seems especially popular with the young set, who like its atmosphere of lively chatter and contemporary d&eacute;cor, with wooden floors, stucco walls, wood tables and chairs, and a busy bar. I do find maps often used in decorating, sometimes on lampshades and sometimes as decoupage. The talented decorator is one who can decoupage lamp bases with blue-and-white nautical charts. At the recent Palm Beach International Boat Show, one could find coffee tables and dining tables of mahogany with nautical charts set into their tops &mdash; perfect for a yacht. They reminded me of the tables at Flanigan&rsquo;s, the casual seafood restaurant and bar just west of the Southern Boulevard Bridge, which has nautical-influenced decor. Back at the boat show, I saw club chairs and dining chairs similarly upholstered in map-printed canvas. Designer Brinsley Matthews in our office recently carried the map feeling into a project at Mackinac Island&rsquo;s Grand Hotel in upper Michigan. He had the ceiling at the Sushi Grand restaurant painted with a map of Asia, with each of the major cities marked with a tiny light. Diners at the restaurant find the map a magical touch. It was painted by one of Palm Beach County&rsquo;s finest, artist Alice Bear Ludwig. Alice also is responsible for the enchanting decorations at Sloan&rsquo;s Ice Cream parlors across the bridge &mdash; an artist of many talents, indeed. So keep an eye out for maps used creatively in d&eacute;cor. Who knows if they&rsquo;ll lead you to some ideas you might incorporate into your own rooms? In any case, you don&rsquo;t have to cross the Euphrates, the Seine or the Tiber to find good design. * Palm Beacher Carleton Varney is president of Dorothy Draper & Co., an international design firm with offices in New York, West Palm Beach, London and White Sulphur Springs, W.Va. co-cooperative units rebounded during the first three months of this year compared to the same period in 2016, according to new sales reports released on behalf of larger real estate agencies that do business on the island. But most of the improvement came in Midtown rather than on the South End. Reports were issued by Douglas Elliman Real Estate, the Corcoran Group, Sotheby&rsquo;s International Realty and Brown Harris Stevens. All four reports analyzed sales in January, February and March, but each used slightly different figures and property categories, making apples-to-apples comparisons difficult. Most of the reports were based solely on sales data taken from the&nbsp;Palm Beach Board of Realtors Multiple Listing Service, although Brown Harris Stevens&rsquo; analysis also included off-market sales, according to Palm Beach-based broker Ava van de Water. The&nbsp;Palm Beach Market Update from Sotheby&rsquo;s noted that 37 Midtown condos sold in the first quarter, an 85 percent jump over the same period in 2016. On the South End, however, the number of first-quarter sales fell slightly, from 39 last year to 38 this year. The total dollar volume for the condos sold, however, increased by nearly 200 percent, year over year &mdash; from $20.4 million to $61.1 million, according to the report from Sotheby&rsquo;s. Average prices declined on the South End, however, while rising in Midtown. The&nbsp;Elliman Report, meanwhile, showed a 38-percent jump in the year-over-year average price paid for condos townwide. Analyst&nbsp;Jonathan Miller, who prepared the analysis for Douglas Elliman, suggested that the &ldquo;surge&rdquo; in condo sales, at least in part, may be due to released pent-up demand from buyers who had not been willing to commit during the contentious election season that led to President Donald Trump&rsquo;s inauguration in January. But Miller cautioned about reading too much into the numbers. &ldquo;It isn&rsquo;t clear if the jump is attributed (mostly) to the election or if it reflects a trend that will lead to a modest increase in (sales) activity over the course of 2017 versus 2016,&rdquo; he said. Corcoran&rsquo;s report and the Brown Harris Stevens anlysis largely attributed the rise in average condo prices to an unusually large&nbsp;$15.5 million deal in late March at 2 North Breakers Row. The sale of Unit S-44 set an oceanfront condo record and marked the second-highest price ever paid for a unit anywhere in Palm Beach. That sale was brokered by Lawrence A. Moens Associates. The reports show the sales numbers for single-family deals were less robust than for condos. According to&nbsp;The Corcoran Report, the number of closed sales dropped from 22 to 21, with a 14-percent decrease in the average price paid for those properties. Median prices were fairly flat, according to Corcoran&rsquo;s analysis. The median is the price at which half of the units sold for more and half for less. All the reports agreed that condos and single-family properties took longer to change hands in the first quarter of this year than those that sold in the same quarter last year. In the single-family category, properties that sold had spent an average of 234 days on the market compared to 74 in 2016, the&nbsp;Elliman Report showed. The condo section of Brown Harris Stevens&rsquo; report, meanwhile, shows that apartments spent 183 days, on average, on the market before selling; for the first quarter of last year, the agency recorded that figure as 126 days. The reports that solely analyzed MLS data, by the way, would have missed the first quarter&rsquo;s biggest single-family deal, an $85 million sale brokered by Moens for 1290 S.         \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  It&rsquo;s not just Democrats who are getting on President Donald Trump about his frequent visits to his tropical White House at Mar-a-Lago. Trump&rsquo;s seven Mar-a-Lago trips in the last 11 weekends are &ldquo;bothering&rdquo; Republican Iowa Sen. Joni Ernst, she told a town hall meeting in her home state Tuesday. Trump has spent about one-fifth of his presidency in Palm Beach County &mdash; hosting two international summits and golfing 14 times. To be precise, the president has spent 419&frac14; hours in Palm Beach County as president &mdash; roughly 17&frac12; days. Most of that time has been spent at Mar-a-Lago in Palm Beach or his nearby Trump International Golf Club in unincorporated West Palm Beach. He also motorcaded to his Trump National Golf Club in Jupiter to play golf with Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe. Trump has also made side trips to Tampa, Melbourne and Orlando that have totaled another 9&frac14; hours in Florida. That&rsquo;s a total of 428&frac12; hours &mdash; nearly 18 days &mdash; in the Sunshine State for Trump, who will have been in office exactly 89 days at noon today. &ldquo;I do wish that he would spend more time in Washington, D.C. That&rsquo;s what we have the White House for. We would love to see more of those State Department visits in Washington, D.C.,&rdquo; Ernst said in an apparent reference to Trump&rsquo;s use of Mar-a-Lago to host Japan&rsquo;s Abe in February and Chinese President Xi Jinping this month. &ldquo;I have not spoken to him about the Florida issue yet, but that is something I think that has been bothering not just me but some other members of our caucus, so I think that is going to be a topic of discussion when we get back to Washington, D.C.,&rdquo; Ernst said. Trips to Mar-a-Lago are estimated to cost the federal government somewhere in the neighborhood of $3 million per weekend, based on a 2016 Government Accountability Office study that found a three-night stay in the Treasure Coast by former President Barack Obama cost $3.6 million, including a stop in Illinois before he arrived in Florida. Palm Beach County taxpayers have spent about $3.7 million on Trump&rsquo;s visits as president-elect and as president, primarily to pay sheriff&rsquo;s deputies overtime to assist the Secret Service. The county is seeking federal reimbursement for the costs. Here&rsquo;s a rundown of Trump&rsquo;s past visits to Mar-a-Lago: &bull; Feb. 3-6: Trump arrived at Palm Beach International Airport at about 4:30 p.m. on Feb. 3 and, after a weekend that included a Super Bowl-watching party at Trump International, he left PBIA around 11 a.m.on Feb. 6 for a trip to Tampa and speech at MacDill Air Force Base. He left Tampa at 2:30 p.m. 66&frac12; hours in Palm Beach County; 3&frac12; additional hours in Florida. &bull; Feb. 10-12: The president and Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe and their wives arrived at PBIA shortly after 5:30 p.m. on Feb. 10. After golfing and a late-night news conference responding to the North Korean missile launch on Feb. 11, Abe left early on Feb. 12 and Trump departed about 5:30 p.m. 48 hours in Palm Beach County. &bull; Feb. 17-20: Air Force One arrived at PBIA about 3:30 p.m. on Feb. 17. Trump took a 3-hour side trip to Melbourne on Feb. 18 for a campaign-style rally . Trump left PBIA at about 4:15 p.m. on Feb. 20. 69&frac34; hours in Palm Beach County; 3 additional hours in Florida. &bull; March 3-5: Air Force One arrived in Orlando about 1 p.m. for a visit by Trump, Education Secretary Betsy DeVos, Florida Gov. Rick Scott and Sen. Marco Rubio to a Catholic school to promote school choice programs. Air Force One then flew to West Palm Beach, arriving at PBIA about 3:45 p.m. on March 3. Trump departed at 4 p.m. on March 5. 48&frac14; hours in Palm Beach County; 2&frac34; additional hours in Florida. &bull; March 17-19: Air Force One arrived at PBIA at 6:44 p.m. with Trump, first lady Melania Trump and son Barron aboard. Trump made two visits to his Trump International Golf Club and was briefly joined at Mar-a-Lago by Vice President Mike Pence on March 18. Departed March 19 at 4:15 p.m. 45&frac12; hours in Palm Beach County. &bull; April 6-9: Arrived at PBIA at 2:50 p.m. on April 6 for summit meeting with Chinese President Xi Jinping; ordered missile strikes on Syria from a situation room in Mar-a-Lago; golfed twice; departed April 9 at 4:30 p.m. 71&frac12; hours in Palm Beach County. &bull; April 13-16: Arrived at PBIA at 6:30 p.m. April 13 for Easter weekend; golfed twice; attended Easter service at Episcopal Church of Bethesda-by-the-Sea; left April 16 at 4:13 p.m. 69&frac34; hours in Palm Beach County. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/national-govt--politics/trump-spends-too-much-time-mar-lago-says-republican-senator/Spa4qibkMie6vXLxmLet5I/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/19/Images/newsEngin.18460334_040917-sd-col-kraft-trump_web.jpg\" title=\"Trump spends too much time at Mar-a-Lago, says Republican senator \" alt=\"Trump spends too much time at Mar-a-Lago, says Republican senator \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/national-govt--politics/trump-spends-too-much-time-mar-lago-says-republican-senator/Spa4qibkMie6vXLxmLet5I/\" target=\"\">Trump spends too much time at Mar-a-Lago, says Republican senator </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nGeorge Bennett, <span class=\"story-source\">Palm Beach Post Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; now permitted, officials say. The U.S. Army Corps of Engineers dredges the Lake Worth Inlet every two years to maintain the navigational channels. The federal agency has been placing the dredged sand on island beaches from just south of the inlet to Angler Avenue. But, the town has been working with the Army Corps over the past year to extend the placement area to Merrain Road. This would allow more sand to be placed on the dry beach instead of the nearshore. Corps contractors now place sand on the dry beach when possible and in the nearshore when the beach template is full. As a condition of placing sand on the dry beach rather than the nearshore, the Corps requires the town to perform certain duties, including sea turtle and hardbottom monitoring, escarpment and shorebird nesting surveys, and tilling. Those activities are part of an agreement with the town and the Florida Department of Environmental Protection. Public Works Director Paul Brazil told the Town Council last week that the town needs to update its contract with DEP to cover the extended placement area. &ldquo;Because the Corps is extending the disposal area, we&rsquo;re entering into a new agreement,&rdquo; he said. Brazil said the town will have the same responsibilities under the new agreement, including mitigation. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think that it is a strong liability of the town, but I really need to emphasize the fact that that liability does exist,&rdquo; he said of the mitigation. &ldquo;It hasn&rsquo;t been an issue in the past. We&rsquo;ve been through several hurricanes and we weren&rsquo;t faced with the liability for mitigation, so I don&rsquo;t see an event that would cause it. But it is possible.&rdquo; The possibly costly mitigation would be for negative impact to hardbottom. Hardbottom is nearshore biodiverse habitat that provides food sources for certain organisms. Brazil said the town already has $10,000 in its capital budget to cover the monitoring costs. The council unanimously approved the agreement. The Army Corps is scheduled to dredge the inlet early next year. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/palm-beach-approves-new-agreement-with-state-for-inlet-sand/6mUbV2Jb6Y4JvkowlZFs3L/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/20/Images/newsEngin.18369317_Corps-tilling-1_web.jpg\" title=\"Palm Beach approves new agreement with state for inlet sand \" alt=\"Palm Beach approves new agreement with state for inlet sand \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/palm-beach-approves-new-agreement-with-state-for-inlet-sand/6mUbV2Jb6Y4JvkowlZFs3L/\" target=\"\">Palm Beach approves new agreement with state for inlet sand </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nAleese Kopf, <span class=\"story-source\">Daily News Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>An updated agreement with the state will allow inlet-dredged sand to be placed on town beaches farther south of what is now permitted, officials say. Though the White House has not confirmed the president&rsquo;s weekend plans, the Federal Aviation Administration had released notices of flight restrictions for a presidential trip by this time in the week ahead of each of Trump&rsquo;s past visits. The lack of such notification at midweek signals it&rsquo;s unlikely the president will fly back into town for an eighth weekend here. When the president has come to town in the past, the FAA typically posts an advisory on Monday notifying pilots that temporary flight restrictions are forthcoming for the airspace over Palm Beach. That advisory usually is followed&nbsp;the next day by the flight restrictions themselves. Also absent: the massive Air Force cargo planes that land at Palm Beach International Airport and have heralded Trump&rsquo;s arrival in the past. Because no details have been released about how Trump will spend his weekend, the president still could make the nearly two-hour flight from Washington, D.C., to Palm Beach International Airport to spend the weekend at his Mar-a-Lago Club. He also could opt to stay at the White House, as he has on six of the 13 weekends since he took office in January. On each of those weekends, the president has visited one of his properties in the area: either his Trump International Golf Club in Sterling, Virginia, or Trump International Hotel in Washington, D.C. Trump has come under fire for the frequency and costs of his trips to Mar-a-Lago, with U.S. Sen. Joni Ernst, R-Iowa,&nbsp;saying this week that it is &ldquo;bothering&rdquo; her. &ldquo;I do wish he would spend more time in Washington,&rdquo; she&nbsp;told a town hall crowd. Trump wrapped up&nbsp;his most recent trip to Palm Beach on Sunday, after arriving last Thursday evening to spend a long Easter weekend with his family at Mar-a-Lago. \n    \n<!-- map content:  A decade ago it would have been no surprise to see a Florida grasshopper sparrow bouncing through the dry grasslands of central Florida. There were hundreds of the bird, which is found nowhere else. Now, there are fewer than 30 in the wild. The Rare Species Conservatory Foundation in Loxahatchee is racing to save the bird. It has 19 birds in its care. Last year, for the first time since the birds arrived in 2015, a pair successfully hatched two broods. &ldquo;This is a beautiful little songbird,&rdquo; said Paul Reillo, founder and president of the foundation. &ldquo;The prairie will be very silent without the Florida grasshopper sparrow.&rdquo; You can&rsquo;t visit the facility, which is closed to the public. But you can learn more about it and other environmental preservation and education groups during the Ann Norton Sculpture Gardens&rsquo; new Earth Day event Saturday at the gardens in West Palm Beach. Dubbed A Partnership for Art, Science and the Natural World, the event will feature: *Sculptor Todd McGrain talking about his Lost Bird Project while Reillo describes the foundation&rsquo;s efforts to save the endangered sparrow, followed by a tour of McGrain&rsquo;s sculptures of extinct North American birds on view in the gardens (11 a.m.-noon) *A panel discussion moderated by gardens President Roger Ward featuring McGrain; Sarah Marmion, manager of Manatee Lagoon - An FPL Eco-Discovery Center in Riviera Beach; Jack Lighton, president and chief executive officer of the Loggerhead Marinelife Center in Juno Beach; and Lew Crampton, president and chief executive officer of the South Florida Science Center and Aquarium in West Palm Beach (2-3 p.m.) *Learning stations and garden tours (3-5 p.m.) The collaborative event &ldquo;will send out a beacon that this is an organization that wants to be active and participate in all things to do with conservation and habitat,&rdquo; Ward said. The late Ann Norton established the gardens in 1977 not just to showcase her monumental outdoor sculptures, but also as a repository of rare palms and cycads, an urban retreat for humanity and a sanctuary for birds and other wildlife. The Rare Species Conservatory Foundation works to preserve imperiled species through field research, captive breeding programs and partnerships worldwide. It concentrates on what it calls &ldquo;flagship species&rdquo; that live in regions rich in biodiversity. To save them, the ecosystem they depend on must be healed, Reillo said. Loggerhead operates a sea turtle hospital and education center and maintains a 9.5-mile stretch of coastline that&rsquo;s the densest sea turtle nesting area in North America. Last year, the organization counted 17,000 nests. Other parts of the state might have only 1,000 nests in 30 miles of coastline, Lighton said. Sea turtles are &ldquo;perfect environmental ambassadors for us because they&rsquo;re so unique to our area,&rdquo; he said. FPL&rsquo;s power plant in Riviera Beach has been a magnet for manatee watchers since the 1960s because of the warm-water outflows that attract the threatened aquatic mammal in winter. The Manatee Lagoon opened in February 2016 adjacent to the power plant with observation areas, exhibits about manatees and the Lake Worth lagoon ecosystem, and activities ranging from yoga classes to junior marinelife labs. Manatees aren&rsquo;t the only creatures visitors can see at the facility. In the weeks before manatee season began in November &ldquo;we had 2,000 sharks every day in the outflow,&rdquo; Marmion said. The science center&rsquo;s displays include include an Everglades exhibit, conservation learning lab, 10,000-gallon aquarium and a giant &ldquo;globe&rdquo; displaying planetary data. Crampton, who worked for the Environmental Protection Agency during the Reagan and elder Bush administrations, welcomes the chance to celebrate Earth Day with other organizations. &ldquo;Every day should be Earth Day,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;We all live on a planet that needs care and affection.&rdquo; The Ann Norton is at 2051 S. Flagler Ave., West Palm Beach. Admission is free to members of the participating organizations with proof of membership and discounted for other visitors. For information, call 832-5328 or visit ansg.org. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/sculpture-gardens-and-partners-launch-new-earth-day-event/ReqVhPTD4zMquk1flSUrRJ/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/20/Images/newsEngin.18439777_earth-day-advance.jpg_web.jpg\" title=\"Sculpture gardens and partners launch new Earth Day event \" alt=\"Sculpture gardens and partners launch new Earth Day event \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/sculpture-gardens-and-partners-launch-new-earth-day-event/ReqVhPTD4zMquk1flSUrRJ/\" target=\"\">Sculpture gardens and partners launch new Earth Day event </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nJan Sjostrom, <span class=\"story-source\">Daily News Arts Editor</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>A decade ago it would have been no surprise to see a Florida grasshopper sparrow bouncing through the dry grasslands of central Florida.      \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  A study out of the University of Florida has put the spotlight back on the Zika virus. In research published in a scientific journal last week, Chelsea Smartt, a molecular biologist at the University of Florida Institute of Food and Agricultural Sciences in Vero Beach, reported finding Zika RNA in Aedes albopictus mosquitoes in Brazil. Public health officials suspected that the Aedes albopictus could spread Zika. The research confirms that suspicion. Aedes aegypti (also known as the yellow fever mosquito) and Aedes albopictus (the Asian tiger mosquito) both abound in Florida, according to the U.S. Centers for Disease Control and Prevention. The CDC doesn&rsquo;t rule out the threat of Aedes albopictus, but the agency says &ldquo;because these mosquitoes feed on animals as well as people, they are less likely to spread viruses like Zika, dengue, chikungunya and other viruses.&rdquo; As for Palm Beach, Deputy Town Manager Jay Boodheshwar said the information about Aedes albopictus is nothing new. Boodheshwar said the town in its public messaging has referred to both types of mosquitoes as potential Zika carriers, and said that both are active in Palm Beach. But the town has focused on aegypti &ldquo;because that is the prevalent mosquito that carries the virus here on the island.&rdquo; Now that the annual hot and rainy season is returning, the town is preparing for another larvicide treatment of all catch basins on public property, Boodheshwar said. The catch basins were treated last fall, but the larvicide tablets are only effective for six months, he said. The public will be notified when the treatment is scheduled, he said. State and federal officials said in December that Zika no longer was being actively transmitted in Florida. But public health officials say Zika is likely to return with summer rains. Tim O&rsquo;Connor, spokesman for the state Department of Health&rsquo;s Palm Beach County unit, said the latest research won&rsquo;t affect the health district&rsquo;s efforts to fight Zika. &ldquo;The main prevention is avoiding mosquito bites,&rdquo; O&rsquo;Connor said. Town Councilwoman Margaret Zeidman, who has helped spearhead Zika education and prevention on the island, said aegypti and albopictus are similar. Both are found in tropical and subtropical habitat. But albopictus, unlike aegypti, tolerates cooler climes, up into the Carolinas and New England, she said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s everyone&rsquo;s personal responsibility to protect themselves from mosquito bites and to get rid of containers that might fill with water,&rdquo; Zeidman said. Containers that hold water should be turned over or treated with a larvicide, she said. Packets are available at Town Hall. To protect from bites, cover your skin with clothing or apply DEET, which is safe for everyone except babies 2 months old or younger. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/research-finds-zika-second-florida-mosquito/GQffQ9vgrsdUMdDacevcYL/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/20/Images/newsEngin.18455999_GettyImages-543392368_web.jpg\" title=\"Research finds Zika in second Florida mosquito \" alt=\"Research finds Zika in second Florida mosquito \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/research-finds-zika-second-florida-mosquito/GQffQ9vgrsdUMdDacevcYL/\" target=\"\">Research finds Zika in second Florida mosquito </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t   \n\t\n\t\n\n\t\t William Kelly , Jeff Ostrowski , <span class=\"story-source\">Daily News Staff Writers</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>A study out of the University of Florida has put the spotlight back on the Zika virus. has created a menu based on culinary styles from Morocco, Spain, France, Italy, Greece and Turkey, with some Asian and Australian dishes and ingredients. He was among our hosts April 11 at the new Mediterranean restaurant in the Esplanade off Worth Avenue. Valencia explained that he grew up in a large extended family where everyday meals felt like celebrations. He has fond memories of his grandmother making blood sausages. His early training included a position with world-renowned chef Alain Ducasse. We had been greeted by Sascha Bennemann, who co-owns Costa with wife Danielle, with a glass of Jeeper Grand Assemblage Brut, Champagne. It was a light golden color, with a nose of gentle fruit aromas layered with freshly baked brioche. This showed in the palate together with a refreshing mineral structure. The Goutorbe family has been making Champagne since the 18th century, but the Jeeper name reflects the donation of a Willys Jeep from the U.S. Army in 1949 in return for support by the family during World War II. This unusual but enjoyable Champagne is indicative of Bennemann&rsquo;s approach to most of the wine list. The Jeeper worked well with a small dish of thinly sliced Hamachi, the Japanese yellowtail, served with micro citrus finger lime, avocado, lemon-y sumac, black garlic jelly and cilantro. It was a good start to the evening. Perfect pairing Valencia presented a whole salt baked branzino. The branzino was removed from the salt casing and served with an arugula emulsion, grilled lemon, and an olive, caper and tomato relish; it was moist but firmand full of flavor. It was served with a 2015 Torre La Moreira Albari&ntilde;o, produced by the Marqu&eacute;s de Vizhoja winery in Spain. This varietal was a pale straw color with a nose of gentle fresh herbs and white fruits that came through in the palate with a crisp, fresh citrus acidity and extended finish &mdash; it was a perfect pairing. Working with Bennemann, Valencia and the owner&rsquo;s plans include adding brunch and Sunday evenings to the options available plus themed family evenings, possibly in the private dining room that opens into the glass-fronted wine cellar. Earlier tastings A glass of the 2015 Skouras Moscofilero was served, from the Skouras winery in Pyrgela in the Peloponnese region of southern Greece. It was a very pale yellow color that was somewhat light but full of flavor, especially citrus tempered by white flowers creating good length. This Moscofilero varietal had been paired with a Caesar salad of romaine hearts, white marinated anchovy, cured egg yolk emulsion, a parmesan crisp and feta. A warm vichyssoise soup appeared, served with potato fondant, fine herbs, charred leek and burned onion (although the charred and burned parts were very gentle and added contrasting flavors and textures to the smooth, silky, almost creamy soup). A bottle of 2015 Bernard Fouquet Domaine des Aubuisieres Vouvray Silex was opened. This Chenin Blanc varietal from the Loire Valley of France was a dry white wine with a crisp mineral backbone to support the plump fruity texture. It was enjoyable and a good value. Syrah and dessert We sampled one of the red wines to see how they compared &mdash; the 2013 Vallin Syrah from the Santa Barbara Appellation in California. It was a rich dark purple color with a good blend of dark fruits and ripe victoria plums enlivened by some pepper and minerals in the palate. The Jeeper reappeared to go with a delicious Baklava dessert. Honey sweet with a crispy texture and nutty flavors it was a good end to an enjoyable, relaxed and friendly tasting. * Paul William Coombs continues to enjoy a successful business career although his passion has always been the world of wine. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/lifestyles/wine/enjoying-tasteful-mix-costa-palm-beach/fmInRgeWseA02Iw0KJADbN/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/20/Images/newsEngin.18447464_042017-Wine---Costa---Wine-Cellar_web.jpg\" title=\"Enjoying a tasteful mix at Costa Palm Beach \" alt=\"Enjoying a tasteful mix at Costa Palm Beach \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/lifestyles/wine/enjoying-tasteful-mix-costa-palm-beach/fmInRgeWseA02Iw0KJADbN/\" target=\"\">Enjoying a tasteful mix at Costa Palm Beach </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nPaul William Coombs, <span class=\"story-source\">Wine Columnist</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>At Costa, Executive Chef David Valencia has created a menu based on culinary styles from Morocco, Spain, France, Italy, Greece and Turkey, with some Asian and Australian dishes and ingredients. 21st century feel-good musical in the wrong hands. Instead it gives us a multi-faceted, larger-than-life lead character, a tuneful score that ranges from soulful to toe-tapping, snappy choreography skillfully integrated into the story, and a book that&rsquo;s filled with humor and pathos. As crafted by choreographer-director Jerry Mitchell, book writer Harvey Fierstein and composer Cyndi Lauper, the show, which is based on the 2005 movie, is an inspirational delight. By the time the finale Raise You Up/Just Be resounded through the theater Tuesday, the Kravis Center crowd was on its feet. In the story, drag queen Lola saves conventional Charlie&rsquo;s failing shoe factory by designing stiletto-heeled boots for cross-dressing men &mdash; and teaches Charlie and his male workers a lesson in tolerance. Timothy Ware shines like a supernova as Lola. He delivers zingers such as &ldquo;As Oscar Wilde said, be yourself. Everyone else is taken&rdquo; with whip-sharp wit and sashays across the stage in flamboyant gowns with confidence. His voice is the equal of any pop music star&rsquo;s and he&rsquo;s a superb actor. When Lola acquiesces to the mores of Northampton, England, where Charlie&rsquo;s factory is situated, and dresses as a man, Ware makes us ache for her when her light dims. By the time he sings the soul-baring Hold Me in Your Heart toward the end of the show we&rsquo;re seduced to do just that. Curt Hansen balances the relationship between Charlie and Lola, the show&rsquo;s linchpin, with a heartfelt portrayal as Charlie grapples with his prejudice and self-doubt. He&rsquo;s particularly moving in the self-lacerating ballad Soul of a Man. Rose Hemingway turns a secondary role as Lauren, the factory worker who gradually wins Charlie&rsquo;s heart, into a sparkler, beginning with a cackle and Lauper-like spin in the number The History of Wrong Guys. The best choreography is reserved for Lola&rsquo;s back-up singer-dancers, the Angels: Joseph Anthony Byrd, Tony d&rsquo;Alelio, John J. Dempsey, Ian Gallagher Fitzgerald, Xavier Reyes and Andy Richardson. They execute flips and cartwheels, swing from railings and strut the stage without missing a beat in sky-high boots. \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n        \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n     \n\n\n        \n            \n            \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n                \n\n\t            \n\t               \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t            \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t                \n\t                    \n\t                    \n\t \n\t            \n\n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n    \n    \n        \n            \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n            \n                \n            \n        \n    \n\n\n\n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n    \n        \n        \n        \n        \n            \n            \n            \n\t\t    \n        \n        \n    \n<!-- map content:  Palm Beach County is one of the nation&rsquo;s wealthiest and most agriculturally rich counties in the country. And yet, it has the highest rate of food insecurity in South Florida. About 200,000 residents, 64,000 of whom are children, do not know where their next meal is coming from, according to the Palm Beach County Food Bank.&nbsp; There is a two-prong approach to the situation. One is the&nbsp;Palm Beach County Food Bank, which does not distribute to individuals but supplies groceries and fresh produce to 175 agencies and programs who feed those in need through food pantries, soup kitchens and other outreach organizations. &ldquo;We are particularly grateful that almost half of the food we receive is produce, gleaned from local farms, which helps us provide much-needed nutritional items that are vital to the health of those we serve,&rdquo; said Karen Erren, executive director of the food bank. The other approach is more community-based, with ready-to-eat meals distributed through soup kitchens and homeless shelters.&nbsp;St. Edward Catholic Church is active in two programs with community outreach organizations. &ldquo;Our parishioners have been very generous,&rdquo; said Monsignor Thomas Klinzing. &ldquo;In addition to cash donations, twice a year one gentleman (who wishes to remain anonymous), sends a tractor-trailer from Michigan to the church.&rdquo; The contents of the truck fill the parish pantry with food service size cans of tomatoes, pork and beans, vegetables, packages of pasta, dry goods, juices, deli meats, cheese, ground beef and sausage. It is used throughout the year for meals prepared on site and distributed the same day. Every Friday morning, for the past 15 years, about a dozen volunteers gather in the parish hall. Their singular mission is to prepare food for bag lunches; the lunches go to St. Ann&rsquo;s in West Palm Beach. Other churches involved with the lunch program include St Patrick Episcopal in West Palm Beach and Cason United Methodist Church in Delray Beach. The hands-on effort was founded by the late&nbsp;Rev. Frank Lechiara. In one hour, the team puts together 500 lunches consisting of two meat and cheese sandwiches, a fruit drink, dessert and candy. &ldquo;The first time I went there, I said just put me wherever you need me,&rdquo; said Patrick Griffin, who had participated in the program several years ago. &ldquo;It was a well-oiled machine. You had to get in the pecking order and stay in your lane.&rdquo; &ldquo;I remember the smell of the bologna, ham and bread. It was a solemn, peaceful experience, like a meditation period while doing God&rsquo;s work. Although I am not a regular churchgoer, it made me feel that I was contributing in other ways.&rdquo; In addition to the lunch program, on Thursday mornings, Frank Merriam and Edward Assad &mdash; whose main function at St. Edward&rsquo;s is to maintain the property &mdash; switch gears. The duo prepares a variety of fresh, hot entrees that are later delivered to the Caring Kitchen in Delray Beach, which is part of&nbsp;CROS Ministries. Their culinary specialties include meatloaf, hot dogs and beans, spaghetti with meat sauce and roast chicken. The two-man team is so well-organized, they can turn out 80 pounds of meatloaf in a couple of hours. If you would like to contribute or volunteer for programs at St. Edward, contact Maggie Shepherd at 832-0400. -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/edward-volunteers-palm-beach-help-efforts-fight-hunger/hwvgWtz37F5xqdyGJxQQJO/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/20/Images/newsEngin.18306136_20170406-pbdn-st.-edward-MM-01_web.jpg\" title=\"St. Edward volunteers in Palm Beach help efforts to fight hunger \" alt=\"St. Edward volunteers in Palm Beach help efforts to fight hunger \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/edward-volunteers-palm-beach-help-efforts-fight-hunger/hwvgWtz37F5xqdyGJxQQJO/\" target=\"\">St. Edward volunteers in Palm Beach help efforts to fight hunger </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nRoberta Sabban</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Palm Beach County is one of the nation’s wealthiest and most agriculturally rich counties in the country. And yet, it has the highest rate of food insecurity in South Florida. The low pressure system is about 700 miles southwest of the Azores and no threat to the U.S. But a storm, even a subtropical storm, is notable this early in the year. Hurricane season doesn&rsquo;t begin until June 1. The storm would be named Arlene. The evolution of Invest #91L's journey in attempting sub-tropical cyclone development via dynamic tropopause heights pic.twitter.com/0XpPwQsHhp &mdash; Michael Ventrice (@MJVentrice) April 18, 2017 The system, dubbed 91L has a window of opportunity to become a subtropical cyclone during the &ldquo;next day or so&rdquo; before it gets absorbed by a larger extratropical cyclone on Thursday. An updated forecast is expected before 2 p.m. today. Looks like an interesting day of work thanks to #AL91! These marginal things are always the toughest decisions. pic.twitter.com/OJNnvx2z1C &mdash; Eric Blake (@EricBlake12) April 19, 2017 &nbsp; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"http://weatherplus.blog.palmbeachdailynews.com/2017/04/19/breaking-chances-of-subtropical-system-increase/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Wires/w2/OldFeeds/2017/04/19/Images/photos.medleyphoto.13981569.jpg\" title=\"Breaking: Chances of subtropical system increase \" alt=\"Breaking: Chances of subtropical system increase \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"http://weatherplus.blog.palmbeachdailynews.com/2017/04/19/breaking-chances-of-subtropical-system-increase/\" target=\"\">Breaking: Chances of subtropical system increase </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nStaff Writer</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>The National Hurricane Center has increased the chances that a sub-tropical system will form in the Central Atlantic to 50 percent over the next 48 hours. \n<!-- map content:  Condominiums and homes in multifamily buildings would fare better than single-family homes in a revised method for determining how much property owners would pay for townwide&nbsp;undergrounding of utilities. The revised assessments were presented to the Town Council last week by Habib Isaac, senior manager with consultant Raftelis Financial Consultants, who contrasted the new figures with those he developed for the town in 2015. The average annual assessment for single-family homes would go up $89 to $1,048. But the annual assessment would drop $48 to $301 for the average condominium, and go down $84 to $327 for units in multifamily buildings. Isaac&rsquo;s assessment method was adopted by the council in 2009, and it was updated in 2015 to keep up with the property database. The latest revisions have not been formally adopted. Before the March 16, 2016, referendum, when voters approved up to $90 million in bonds for the undergrounding project, the property owners could review on the town&rsquo;s website an estimate of how much they and other owners would pay each year for 30 years. The assessment method reflects the benefits each property would receive &mdash; in terms of improved aesthetics, safety and system reliability, Isaac said. Those with the most benefit &ldquo;units&rdquo; assigned pay more. There are some minor changes this time; larger lot sizes pay more than before for safety and aesthetic benefits, he said. &ldquo;It appears to me there is huge savings to the condos as opposed to the single-family homes,&rdquo; Mayor Gail Coniglio said. The estimates do not include &ldquo;soft costs&rdquo; such as legal fees and the tax collector&rsquo;s collection fee, Isaac said. Once known, those will be included. Councilwoman Julie Araskog suggested and the rest of the council agreed to request that, in cases where a property has multiple above-ground utility boxes on it, the assessment account for that. A resolution will be brought before the council May 9. If the council decides to adopt the plan a public hearing would be held in July or August, said assessment attorney Heather Encinosa. Some condo residents have said that under the assessments they pay a disproportionately greater share than they would if the project was paid for with property taxes. Bradford said in an email Tuesday that the assessment revisions are &ldquo;primarily related to formalizing the informal changes the Town Council requested before the referendum in March 2016. I consider those changes to be addressing primarily the cost concerns related to condominiums.&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/condo-owners-assessments-for-palm-beach-utility-project-could-fall/hT8rjWYbz5f1OuBa9t67SP/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/19/Images/newsEngin.18439479_022816-everglades-1_web.jpg\" title=\"Condo owners’ assessments for Palm Beach utility project could fall \" alt=\"Condo owners’ assessments for Palm Beach utility project could fall \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/condo-owners-assessments-for-palm-beach-utility-project-could-fall/hT8rjWYbz5f1OuBa9t67SP/\" target=\"\">Condo owners’ assessments for Palm Beach utility project could fall </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t  \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t \n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\n\t\t\n\t\nWilliam Kelly</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Condominiums and homes in multifamily buildings would fare better than single-family homes in a revised method for determining how much property owners would pay for townwide undergrounding of utilities. \n<!-- map content:  Lakefront residents are looking forward to an Intracoastal dredging project this summer that will allow them access to their private docks at low tide. After a short pause to work out a dump site for the dredged muck, the project is on track and scheduled to begin this summer. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m imploring the council to go forward as quickly as possible,&rdquo; Mayor Gail Coniglio said at a meeting last week. &ldquo;These residents have worked diligently and way too long to get these approvals.&rdquo; The Town Council unanimously approved modifying its contract with Coston Marine Services to change the location of the dump site and slightly extend one of the channels. Palm Beach Intracoastal Neighbors, a group of about 60 lakefront residents, already has donated an extra $333,000 to the town to cover the changes. The group is paying for the $1.3 million project, but the town is handling the permitting process. John Scarpa attended the meeting last week to thank the council on behalf of his neighbors. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been three years,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re excited to get this done. We look forward to this project getting underway soon.&rdquo; The project is to dredge three waterways from Midtown to the South End. The north channel is between Everglades Island and the Southern Boulevard Causeway; the central one is south of the causeway to Widener&rsquo;s Curve; and the south waterway is south of Widener&rsquo;s to north of Sloan&rsquo;s Curve. The contractor had planned to dump the dredged material in John&rsquo;s Hole, an underwater site on the West Palm Beach side of the lagoon west of Sloan&rsquo;s Curve and John&rsquo;s Island near the C-51 canal. &ldquo;The property owners adjacent to that hole have expressed concern to the regulatory agencies,&rdquo; Public Works Director Paul Brazil told the council. &ldquo;We believe that it is permittable &hellip; however we also acknowledge that it&rsquo;s going to be challenging to get that permit because of the public pushback.&rdquo; Instead, Palm Beach residents are paying the extra money to haul the dredged material to Bonefish Cove. Bonefish Cove is a county &ldquo;living shorelines&rdquo; project to raise the elevation of the lagoon and create three small mangrove islands and oyster reefs between the Lake Worth Bridge and Hypoluxo Island. collected more than 75 pounds of trash from the South End on Saturday. &ldquo;Unfortunately, we&rsquo;re victims of ocean pollution,&rdquo; said Diane Buhler, founder of Friends of Palm Beach. Around 51 volunteers gathered on Dolphin Road to pick up garbage along the South End shore. It was part of the  national Great American Cleanup program where people get together to clean and improve their communities. Earlier this year, residents were complaining to town officials about the amount of trash and seaweed washing onshore. Buhler said she sees lots of single-use plastic such as utensils and bottles, but bottle caps are most common. According to the Ocean Conservancy, more than 18 million pounds of trash were collected last year. That&rsquo;s equivalent to the weight of 437 whale sharks. The animals can&rsquo;t distinguish the algae-wrapped plastic, and eat it. Then, we eat the animals, Buhler said. &ldquo;We in turn ingest it from grouper, swordfish, snapper and mahi mahi,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not necessarily were eating a piece of plastic, but the plastic derives from oil, so the toxins that are leaching from these plastics are what we&rsquo;re eating.&rdquo; Buhler has been doing monthly beach cleanups on the island for more than four years. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s limited beach access on Palm Beach, so I have a few key spots where the parking is doable,&rdquo; she said. People of all ages volunteer, but Buhler enjoys showing kids the importance of keeping the environment clean. &ldquo;When they fill up a Flanigan&rsquo;s cup with just plastic caps,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;It makes an impact on them.&rdquo; Young people go home and persuade their parents to buy liter bottles instead of smaller ones, and switch to reusable bottles. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m all about solutions,&rdquo; Buhler said. &ldquo;If you complain about all the trash, why don&rsquo;t you just pick it up?&rdquo; -->\n<article class=\"story cm-channel-story cm-story cm-image\">\n\t    <a href=\"/news/local/friends-palm-beach-clean-south-end-beaches/6E5WqXNr5RUTrvvXVdZqzL/\" class=\"story-photo-link\" target=\"\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<img src=\"/rf/image_inline/Pub/p8/PalmBeachDailyNews/2017/04/19/Images/newsEngin.18433099_20170415-BEACH-CLEANUP_web.jpg\" title=\"Friends of Palm Beach clean up at South End beaches \" alt=\"Friends of Palm Beach clean up at South End beaches \">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<span class=\"\"></span>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t</figure>\n\t\t\t\t\t</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div class=\"story-tease-content\">\n\t\t\t\t\t<h3 class=\"story-title\">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<a href=\"/news/local/friends-palm-beach-clean-south-end-beaches/6E5WqXNr5RUTrvvXVdZqzL/\" target=\"\">Friends of Palm Beach clean up at South End beaches </a>\n\t\t\t\t\t</h3>\n\t\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t        <p class=\"story-byline\">\n    \n\t    by\n\t\t\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\n\t\n\t\t\n\t\n\t\n\t\nCarla Trivino, <span class=\"story-source\">Daily News Staff Writer</span>\n\t</p>\n<div class=\"story-text cm-truncate\" style=\"word-wrap: break-word; visibility: visible;\"><div>Friends of Palm Beach cleanup volunteers collected more than 75 pounds of trash from the South End on Saturday. “Unfortunately, we’re victims of ocean pollution,” said Diane Buhler, founder of Friends of Palm Beach. Glickstein said he plans to ask the court to approve releasing money so he can have the $350,000 repairs completed and can pay back the $190,000 owed to the town in fines. The Code Enforcement Board approved a 120-day fine abatement for the property last month.
